Submodularity is an important property of set functions and has been extensively studied in the literature [1, 2, 3, 4]. It models set functions that exhibit a diminishing returns property, where the marginal value of adding an element to a set decreases as the set expands (i.e., contains more elements). The notion of submodularity has been generalized to considering sequence functions, where the order of adding elements plays a crucial role and determines the function value; the generalized notion is called sequence (or string) submodularity [5, 6, 7, 8, 9, 10, 11]. Several real-world applications, including machine learning based recommendation systems, ads allocation, and automation and control, involve the selection of elements in sequence.In this paper, we study a new problem of robust sequence submodular maximization with cardinality constraints. The robustness is against the removal of a subset of elements in the selected sequence (e.g., due to malfunctions or adversarial attacks).To motivate the new problem studied in this paper, we begin with the discussions about two concrete applications (sensor activation and movie recommendation) and use them to illustrate the key differences between set functions and sequence functions.

Sensor Activation. Consider the problem of sensor activation for moving target detection [5], where the objective is to sequentially activate a certain number of sensors to maximize the probability of detecting a moving target. Suppose that each sensor covers a certain area. Without any prior knowledge of the target location or the probability of detection at each location, it is plausible to maximize the total area covered by the activated sensors. If the coverage area of each sensor remains constant over time, then it is sufficient to decide which subset of sensors to activate without concerning the order in which the sensors are activated. This scenario can typically be modeled as maximizing a (set) submodular function.

In practice, however, the coverage area of each sensor may decay over time for several reasons, such as battery decay and corrosive environment. Accounting for such factors, the coverage area of a sensor may be modeled as a decreasing function, e.g., in the form of C​e−t/T𝐶superscript𝑒𝑡𝑇Ce^{-t/{T}}, where C𝐶C is the initial coverage area, T𝑇T is the sensor’s lifetime, and t=0,1,…𝑡01…t=0,1,\dots is the time index. In this scenario, the sequence in which the sensors are activated is of critical importance. The activation sequence determines the total coverage area and thus impacts the probability of successful detection.

The above problem becomes more challenging if some sensors may be malfunctioning after they are activated or, even worse, if there is an adversary that may attack some sensors and render them non-working. Depending on how critical the application scenario is, one must ensure resilience of the sensor activation plan such that a certain performance (i.e., probability of successful detection) can still be guaranteed even in the worst-case failure scenario.

Movie Recommendation. Consider that a service provider (e.g., Netflix) would like to recommend movies to a user [6, 12].It is common that the service provider recommends movies of similar flavor to a user. To some user, however, the incremental level of entertainment of watching a movie may decrease if the user had watched more similar movies, which exhibits a diminishing returns property.

In reality, however, the order in which the movies are recommended to the user may also impact how the user perceives a specific movie. In fact, movie recommendation and TV show recommendation have been modeled as sequence functions in [6] and [12], respectively. As noted in the motivating example in [12], if the model determines that the user might be interested in The Lord of the Rings series, then recommending The Return of the King first and TheFellowship of the Ring last could make the user unsatisfied with an otherwise excellent recommendation. Moreover, the user may not watch all the recommended videos possibly because the user has already watched some of them or does not like them (e.g., due to low ratings and/or unfavorable reviews).

While the problem of submodular maximization is generally NP-hard due to its combinatorial nature, the property of submodularity has been exploited to design efficient approximation algorithms. Since the seminal work in [1], it has been well known that a simple greedy algorithm and its variants can achieve an optimal approximation ratio111The approximation ratio is defined as the ratio of the objective value achieved by an algorithm over that achieved by an optimal algorithm. of (1−1/e)11𝑒(1-1/e) in various settings. Specifically, a variant of the greedy algorithm has also been shown to achieve the same approximation ratio for the problem of sequence submodular maximization [7]. Recently, robust versions of the submodular maximization problem have aroused a lot of research interests (e.g., [13, 16, 15]). The focus of these studies is on selecting a set of elements that is robust against the removal of a subset of them.

In this paper, we take one step further and consider a robust version of the sequence submodular maximization problem. The goal is to select a sequence of elements (i.e., elements in a specific order) with cardinality constraints such that the value of the sequence function is maximized when a certain number of the selected elements may be removed.In Table 1, we position our work along with the literature on submodular maximization. The generalization to robust sequence submodular maximization introduces new challenges. As far as sequence functions are concerned, not only do the notions of submodularity and monotonicity involve variants of subtle yet critical differences, but the design and analysis of robust algorithms are also faced with novel technical difficulties, which render the proofs more challenging. In the sequel, we elaborate on these unique challenges.

First, there are two definitions of submodularity for set functions: (i) the marginal value of adding an element to a set decreases as the set expands; (ii) the marginal value of adding a set to another set decreases as the latter set expands. It is trivial to show that these two definitions are equivalent.Replacing “set” with “sequence” in the above definitions gives two similar definitions for sequence functions. We will show that while (ii) still trivially implies (i) for sequence functions, the opposite does not hold in general.This leads to the following important question: Which definition of submodularity should one consider for sequence functions? Interestingly, while the weaker form (i) is sufficient for establishing provable approximation guarantees for non-robust sequence submodular maximization, one needs the stronger form (ii) to obtain similar results for the robust counterpart.

Second, while monotonicity is a straightforward notion for set functions (i.e., the value of a set function does not decrease as the set expands), there are two directions of monotonicity for sequence functions: forward monotonicity and backward monotonicity. Forward (resp., backward) monotonicity means that adding a sequence to the end (resp., beginning) of another sequence does not decrease the overall value. Both monotonicity properties are important to proving performance guarantees.

Third, the impact of removing an element from a sequence depends both on the element itself and on its position in the sequence. This makes the robust algorithms designed for set functions inapplicable here and calls for new robust algorithms that are better suited for sequence functions. Besides, one needs stronger assumptions to proceed with performance analysis for sequence functions. Therefore, it is more important to prove performance guarantees under weaker assumptions based on approximate versions of submodularity and monotonicity, which are more likely to hold in practice.

Due to these unique challenges, it is unclear what conditions are sufficient for establishing provable approximation ratios for robust sequence submodular maximization, how to design efficient and robust algorithms, and how to prove performance guarantees for the designed algorithms. We aim to answer these questions in this paper. Our contributions are summarized as follows.•To the best of our knowledge, this is the first work that considers the problem of robust sequence submodular maximization. It is well known that the traditional (set) submodular maximization problem is already NP-hard. Accounting for sequence functions and robustness guarantees adds extra layers of difficulty, as the submodular and monotone properties of sequence functions involve variants with subtle yet critical differences.•To address these unique challenges, we design two robust greedy algorithms for maximizing a forward-monotone, backward-monotone, and sequence-submodular function with cardinality constraints. While one algorithm achieves a constant approximation ratio but is robust only against the removal of a subset of contiguous elements, the other is robust against the removal of an arbitrary subset of the selected elements but requires a stronger assumption and achieves an approximation ratio that depends on the number of the removed elements. Although our proposed greedy algorithms are quite intuitive, the theoretical analysis is more challenging, and the presented approximation guarantees are highly nontrivial.•We consider different definitions of submodularity and monotonicity and investigate their impacts on the derived theoretical results.Our study reveals that compared to set functions, one needs more properties of sequence functions to establish similar approximation results.On the other hand, we introduce general versions of such properties, such as approximate sequence submodularity and approximate backward monotonicity, and leverage them to prove approximation results of the proposed algorithms under weaker assumptions, which are more likely to hold in practice.We hope that this work serves as an important first step towards thedesign and analysis of efficient algorithms for robust sequence submodular maximization, which is worth further investigation through empirical evaluations for specific applications.

Due to space limitations, we provide all the proofs in the supplementary document.

Before we define the problem of sequence submodular maximization, which was first considered in [7], we introduce some important definitions. Consider a sequence function h:ℋ​(𝒱)→ℝ+:ℎ→ℋ𝒱superscriptℝh:\mathcal{H}(\mathcal{V})\rightarrow\mathbb{R}^{+}, where ℝ+superscriptℝ\mathbb{R}^{+} is the set of non-negative real numbers. Similarly, function hℎh is said to be element-sequence-submodular if for all v∈𝒱𝑣𝒱v\in\mathcal{V}, we haveh​((v)|S1)≥h​((v)|S2),∀S1,S2∈ℋ​(𝒱)​ such that ​S1⪯S2.formulae-sequenceℎconditional𝑣subscript𝑆1ℎconditional𝑣subscript𝑆2for-allsubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2ℋ𝒱 such that subscript𝑆1precedes-or-equalssubscript𝑆2h((v)|S_{1})\geq h((v)|S_{2}),~{}\forall S_{1},S_{2}\in\mathcal{H}(\mathcal{V})~{}\text{ such that }S_{1}\preceq S_{2}.(3)

From the above definitons, it is easy to see that a sequence-submodular function must also be element-sequence-submodular. However, an element-sequence-submodular function may not necessarily be sequence-submodular; we provide such an example in our supplementary material. This is in contrary to submodular (set) functions, for which one can easily verify that similar definitions of Eqs. (2) and (3) imply each other. Although it is noted (without a proof) in [5] that using an induction argument, one can show that an element-sequence-submodular function must also be sequence-submodular, we find this claim false due to the counterexample we find (see our supplementary material).

Also, function hℎh is said to be forward-monotone ifh​(S1⊕S2)≥h​(S1),∀S1,S2∈ℋ​(𝒱),formulae-sequenceℎdirect-sumsubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆1for-allsubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2ℋ𝒱h(S_{1}\oplus S_{2})\geq h(S_{1}),~{}\forall S_{1},S_{2}\in\mathcal{H}(\mathcal{V}),(4)and is said to be backward-monotone ifh​(S1⊕S2)≥h​(S2),∀S1,S2∈ℋ​(𝒱).formulae-sequenceℎdirect-sumsubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2for-allsubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2ℋ𝒱h(S_{1}\oplus S_{2})\geq h(S_{2}),~{}\forall S_{1},S_{2}\in\mathcal{H}(\mathcal{V}).(5)For the sensor activation example we discussed in the introduction, forward monotonicity (resp., backward monotonicity) means that adding a sequence of sensors to the end (resp., the beginning) of another sequence of sensors does not reduce the total coverage area. We will later introduce approximate versions of sequence submodularity and backward monotonicity and generalize the theoretical results under weaker assumptions based on such generalized properties (see Section 4).

The problem of selecting a sequence S∈ℋ​(𝒱)𝑆ℋ𝒱S\in\mathcal{H}(\mathcal{V}) with an objective of maximizing function hℎh with cardinality constraints (i.e., selecting no more than k𝑘k elements for k>0𝑘0k>0) can be formulated asmaxS∈ℋ​(𝒱),|S|≤kh​(S).formulae-sequence𝑆ℋ𝒱𝑆𝑘ℎ𝑆\displaystyle\underset{S\in\mathcal{H}(\mathcal{V}),~{}|S|\leq k}{\max}\quad h(S).(P𝑃P)

Next, we propose a robust version of Problem (P𝑃P), which accounts for the removal of some of the selected elements. Consider τ≤k𝜏𝑘\tau\leq k. Without loss of generality, we assume k>1𝑘1k>1 for Problem (R𝑅R). In the next section, we discuss the challenges of Problem (R𝑅R) and present the proposed robust algorithms.

We begin with a discussion about the non-robust sequence submodular maximization problem (Problem (P𝑃P)), through which we provide useful insights into the understanding of the challenges of Problem (R𝑅R). Although Problem (P𝑃P) is NP-hard, it can be approximately solved using a simple Sequence Submodular Greedy (SSG) algorithm [7]. Under the SSG algorithm, we begin with an empty sequence S𝑆S; in each iteration, we choose an element that leads to the largest marginal value with respect to S𝑆S and append it to sequence S𝑆S, i.e., S=S⊕arg​maxv∈𝒱∖𝒱​(S)⁡h​((v)|S)𝑆direct-sum𝑆subscriptargmax𝑣𝒱𝒱𝑆ℎconditional𝑣𝑆S=S\oplus\operatorname*{arg\,max}_{v\in\mathcal{V}\setminus\mathcal{V}(S)}h((v)|S). We repeat the above procedure until k𝑘k elements have been selected. It has been shown in [7] that the SSG algorithm achieves an approximation ratio of (1−1/e)11𝑒(1-1/e) for maximizing a forward-monotone, backward-monotone, and element-sequence-submodular function with cardinality constraints.

Although the SSG algorithm approximately solves Problem (P𝑃P), it can perform very poorly if one directly applies it to solving its robust counterpart (Problem (R𝑅R)). The intuition behind this is the following. The SSG algorithm tends to concentrate the value of the selected sequence on the first few elements. Selecting elements in this manner leaves the overall sequence vulnerable as removing some of these elements would have a high impact on the overall value. Consider the following example, where we assume τ=1𝜏1\tau=1 for simplicity. Let 𝒱1={v}subscript𝒱1𝑣\mathcal{V}_{1}=\{v\}, 𝒱2={u1,…,un}subscript𝒱2subscript𝑢1…subscript𝑢𝑛\mathcal{V}_{2}=\{u_{1},\dots,u_{n}\}, 𝒱3={w1,…,wn}subscript𝒱3subscript𝑤1…subscript𝑤𝑛\mathcal{V}_{3}=\{w_{1},\dots,w_{n}\}, and 𝒱=𝒱1∪𝒱2∪𝒱3𝒱subscript𝒱1subscript𝒱2subscript𝒱3\mathcal{V}=\mathcal{V}_{1}\cup\mathcal{V}_{2}\cup\mathcal{V}_{3}. Assume h​((v))=1ℎ𝑣1h((v))=1, h​((ui))=1/nℎsubscript𝑢𝑖1𝑛h((u_{i}))=1/n for all ui∈𝒱2subscript𝑢𝑖subscript𝒱2u_{i}\in\mathcal{V}_{2}, and h​((wi))=ϵℎsubscript𝑤𝑖italic-ϵh((w_{i}))=\epsilon for all wi∈𝒱3subscript𝑤𝑖subscript𝒱3w_{i}\in\mathcal{V}_{3}, where ϵitalic-ϵ\epsilon is an arbitrarily small positive number. Also, assume that for any S1,S2∈ℋ​(𝒱)subscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2ℋ𝒱S_{1},S_{2}\in\mathcal{H}(\mathcal{V}) such that v∈𝒱​(S1)𝑣𝒱subscript𝑆1v\in\mathcal{V}(S_{1}) and v∉𝒱​(S2)𝑣𝒱subscript𝑆2v\notin\mathcal{V}(S_{2}), we have h​((ui)|S1)=0ℎconditionalsubscript𝑢𝑖subscript𝑆10h((u_{i})|S_{1})=0 and h​((ui)|S2)=1/nℎconditionalsubscript𝑢𝑖subscript𝑆21𝑛h((u_{i})|S_{2})=1/n for all ui∈𝒱2subscript𝑢𝑖subscript𝒱2u_{i}\in\mathcal{V}_{2} and h​((wi)|S1)=h​((wi)|S2)=ϵℎconditionalsubscript𝑤𝑖subscript𝑆1ℎconditionalsubscript𝑤𝑖subscript𝑆2italic-ϵh((w_{i})|S_{1})=h((w_{i})|S_{2})=\epsilon for all wi∈𝒱3subscript𝑤𝑖subscript𝒱3w_{i}\in\mathcal{V}_{3}. Suppose k=n𝑘𝑛k=n. Then, the SSG algorithm will select v𝑣v as the first element and select the subsequent n−1𝑛1n-1 elements from 𝒱3subscript𝒱3\mathcal{V}_{3}. The value of the selected sequence will be 1+(n−1)​ϵ1𝑛1italic-ϵ1+(n-1)\epsilon. If element v𝑣v is removed, then the value of the remaining sequence will be (n−1)​ϵ𝑛1italic-ϵ(n-1)\epsilon, which can be arbitrarily small. In contrast, a sequence consisting of n𝑛n elements from 𝒱2subscript𝒱2\mathcal{V}_{2} will be robust against the removal of any element. This is because the overall value is equally distributed across all the elements in the sequence and the value of the sequence after removing any element is (n−1)/n𝑛1𝑛(n-1)/n.

The above example shows that the SSG algorithm may perform arbitrarily bad for Problem (R𝑅R). To that end, we propose two greedy algorithms that can address this limitation and ensure robustness of the selected sequence for Problem (R𝑅R). First, we propose an algorithm that achieves a constant approximation ratio but is robust only against the removal of τ𝜏\tau contiguous elements (Section 3.1). Then, we further propose an algorithm that works in a general setting without the contiguous restriction and is robust against the removal of an arbitrary subset of τ𝜏\tau selected elements, but it requires a stronger assumption and achieves an approximation ratio that depends on the number of removed elements (Section 3.2).

In this subsection, we wish to design an algorithm that is robust against the removal of τ𝜏\tau contiguous elements. The assumption of the removal of contiguous elements can model a spatial relationship such as sensors in close proximity or a temporal relationship such as consecutive episodes of a TV show. We design a variant of the SSG algorithm that approximately solves Problem (R𝑅R). The algorithm is presented in Algorithm 1. As we discussed earlier, the limitation of the SSG algorithm is that the selected sequence is vulnerable because the overall value might be concentrated in the first few elements. Algorithm 1 is motivated by this key observation and works in two steps. In Step 1, we select a sequence S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} of τ𝜏\tau elements from elements in 𝒱𝒱\mathcal{V} in a greedy manner as in SSG. In Step 2, we select another sequence S2subscript𝑆2S_{2} of k−τ𝑘𝜏k-\tau elements from elements in 𝒱∖𝒱​(S1)𝒱𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{V}\setminus\mathcal{V}(S_{1}), again in a greedy manner as in SSG. Note that when we select sequence S2subscript𝑆2S_{2}, we perform the greedy selection as if sequence S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} does not exist at all. This ensures that the value of the final sequence S=S1⊕S2𝑆direct-sumsubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2S=S_{1}\oplus S_{2} is not concentrated in either S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} or S2subscript𝑆2S_{2}. The complexity of Algorithm 1 is O​(k​V)𝑂𝑘𝑉O(kV), which is in terms of the number of function evaluations used in the algorithm.

We first state the following assumption that is needed for deriving the main results in this subsection.

In Theorem 1, we state the approximation result of Algorithm 1 in a special case of τ=1𝜏1\tau=1. We consider this special case for two reasons: (i) it is easier to explain the key ideas in the proof of this special case; (ii) we can prove better approximation ratios in this special case, which may not be obtained from the analysis in the case of 1≤τ≤k1𝜏𝑘1\leq\tau\leq k.

In Theorem 2, we state the approximation result of Algorithm 1 in the case of 1≤τ≤k1𝜏𝑘1\leq\tau\leq k.

In this subsection, we wish to design an algorithm that is robust against the removal of an arbitrary subset of τ𝜏\tau selected elements, which are not necessarily contiguous.One weakness of Algorithm 1 is that the value of the selected sequence could be concentrated in the first few elements of subsequences S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} and S2subscript𝑆2S_{2}. If we allow the removal of an arbitrary subset of τ𝜏\tau selected elements, the removal of the first few elements of subsequences S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} and S2subscript𝑆2S_{2} could leave the remaining sequence with little or no value. By considering a special case in Section 3.1 where the removed elements are restricted to be contiguous, we have managed to prevent such worst case from happening. However, the problem becomes more challenging when we consider a more general case without such a restriction. In the following, we propose an algorithm that is robust against the removal of an arbitrary subset of τ𝜏\tau selected elements, but it requires a stronger assumption and achieves an approximation ratio that depends on the value of τ𝜏\tau. This new algorithm is presented in Algorithm 2.

Similar to Algorithm 1, Algorithm 2 works in two steps. However, there is a subtle yet critical difference in Step 1, which is the key to ensuring robustness in the general case. Specifically, in Step 1 of Algorithm 2, we select a sequence S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} of τ𝜏\tau elements from 𝒱𝒱\mathcal{V} by iteratively choosing an element v𝑣v in a greedy manner, based on its absolute value h​((v))ℎ𝑣h((v)) instead of its marginal value h​((v)|S1)ℎconditional𝑣subscript𝑆1h((v)|S_{1}) as in Algorithm 1. We then select a sequence S2subscript𝑆2S_{2} in Step 2, which is the same as that of Algorithm 1. The final output is S=S1⊕S2𝑆direct-sumsubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2S=S_{1}\oplus S_{2}. Algorithm 2 also has a complexity of O​(k​V)𝑂𝑘𝑉O(kV).

Before we state the approximation results of Algorithm 2, we introduce a generalized definition of sequence submodularity.Function hℎh is said to be general-sequence-submodular if for all S3∈ℋ​(𝒱)subscript𝑆3ℋ𝒱S_{3}\in\mathcal{H}(\mathcal{V}), we haveh​(S3|S1)≥h​(S3|S2),∀S1,S2∈ℋ​(𝒱)​such that S1 is a subsequence of S2.formulae-sequenceℎconditionalsubscript𝑆3subscript𝑆1ℎconditionalsubscript𝑆3subscript𝑆2for-allsubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2ℋ𝒱such that S1 is a subsequence of S2h(S_{3}|S_{1})\geq h(S_{3}|S_{2}),~{}\forall S_{1},S_{2}\in\mathcal{H}(\mathcal{V})~{}\text{such that $S_{1}$ is a subsequence of $S_{2}$}.(6)Note that S1⪯S2precedes-or-equalssubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2S_{1}\preceq S_{2} implies that S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} is a subsequence of S2subscript𝑆2S_{2}, but not vice versa. Therefore, the general sequence submodularity defined above generalizes the sequence submodularity defined in Eq. (2) as a special case with S1⪯S2precedes-or-equalssubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2S_{1}\preceq S_{2}.Next, we state Assumption 2 and Theorem 3.

In this section, we introduce generalized versions of sequence submodularity and backward monotonicity, which are called approximate sequence submodularity and approximate backward monotonicity. Then, we show that Algorithms 1 and 2 can also approximately solve Problem (R𝑅R) under weaker assumptions based on such generalized properties.

We are now ready to state the generalized approximation results of Algorithm 1 under Assumptions 3 and 4, respectively.

Finally, we state the approximation result of Algorithms 2 under Assumption 5.

Since the seminal work in [1], submodular maximization has been extensively studied in the literature. Several efficient approximation algorithms have been developed for maximizing a submodular set function in various settings (e.g., [1, 2, 3, 4]). The concept of sequence (or string) submodularity for sequence functions is a generalization of submodularity, which has been introduced recently in several studies (e.g., [5, 6, 7, 8, 9, 10, 11, 26]). In [7], it has been shown that a simple greedy algorithm can achieve an approximation ratio of (1−1/e)11𝑒(1-1/e) for maximizing a forward-monotone, backward-monotone, and element-sequence-submodular function.

On the other hand, robust versions of submodular maximization has been considered in some recent studies (e.g., [13, 14, 15, 16]), where the focus is on selecting a set of elements that is robust against the removal of a subset of them. In [13], the authors propose the first algorithm with a constant approximation ratio for the problem of robust submodular maximization with cardinality constraints, where the selected set is of size k𝑘k and the robustness is against the removal of any τ𝜏\tau elements of the selected set. The constant approximation ratio derived in [13] is valid as long as the number of removed elements is small compared to the selected set (i.e., τ=o​(k)𝜏𝑜𝑘\tau=o(\sqrt{k})). An extension that guarantees the same constant approximation ratio but allows the removal of a larger number of elements (i.e., τ=o​(k)𝜏𝑜𝑘\tau=o(k)) is presented in [14]. Another algorithm that allows the removal of an arbitrary number of elements under a mild assumption is presented in [15]. The work in [16] relaxes the restriction on τ𝜏\tau, but the achieved approximation ratio depends on the value of τ𝜏\tau. The work in [17] considers the same problem under different types of constraints, such as matroid and knapsack constraints. The work in [18, 19] extends the work in [16] to a multi-stage setting, where the decision at one stage takes into account the failures that happened in the previous stages. Other extensions that consider fairness and privacy issues are studied in [20, 21]. It is unclear whether all of these algorithms for robust set submodularity can be properly extended to our problem, as converting a set into a sequence could result in an arbitrarily bad performance. Even if so, it is more likely that establishing their approximation guarantees would require a more sophisticated analysis, which calls for more in-depth investigations. Note that the analysis of our simple greedy algorithms is already very sophisticated.

In [22], a different notion of robustness is considered, which is referred to as maximizing the minimum of multiple submodular functions. This work proposes a bicriterion approximation algorithm for the studied problem with cardinality constraints. Moreover, the work in [23, 24, 25] extends that of [22] to accommodate a wide variety of constraints, including matroid and knapsack constraints.The work in [27] develops an approximation algorithm for robust non-submodular maximization, using other characterizations such as the submodularity ratio and the inverse curvature. The work in [12] introduces the idea of adaptive sequence submodular maximization, which aims to utilize the feedback obtained in previous iterations to improve the current decision. Note that while the work in [6, 12, 26] assumes that the sequential relationship among elements is encoded as a directed acyclic graph, we consider a general setting without such structures. It would indeed be interesting to explore our algorithms when the sequential relationship is encoded in a specific graphical form.

In this paper, we investigated a new problem of robust sequence submodular maximization. We discussed the unique challenges introduced by considering sequence functions and ensuring robustness guarantees.To address these novel challenges, we proposed two robust greedy algorithms and proved that they can achieve certain approximation ratios for the considered problem, assuming forward-monotone, backward-monotone, and sequence-submodular functions. We further introduced approximate versions of sequence submodularity and backward monotonicity and showed that the proposed algorithms can also provide performance guarantees under a larger class of weaker assumptions based on such generalized properties. Our future work includes developing more efficient algorithms with better approximation ratios in the general settings and investigating the possibility of obtaining similar results under the assumption of generalized/approximate forward monotonicity.

This work contributes to the state-of-the-art theory of submodular optimization. The proposed algorithms and the presented approximation results can be applied to real-world applications where the stated assumptions of sequence submodularity and monotonicity or their approximate versions are satisfied. Several real-world applications, including machine learning based recommendation systems, ads allocation, and automation and control, involve the selection of elements in sequence.

In [5], it is noted (without a proof) that an element-sequence-submodular function must also be sequence-submodular. However, we find this claim false and present a counterexample in Table 2 to show that an element-sequence-submodular function is not necessarily sequence-submodular. In the presented example, it is easy to verify that function hℎh is element-sequence-submodular, i.e., Eq. (3) is satisfied; however, it is not sequence-submodular, i.e., Eq. (2) is not satisfied. Specifically, we have h​((v2,v3)|())<h​((v2,v3)|(v1))ℎconditionalsubscript𝑣2subscript𝑣3ℎconditionalsubscript𝑣2subscript𝑣3subscript𝑣1h((v_{2},v_{3})|())<h((v_{2},v_{3})|(v_{1})) due toh​((v2,v3)|())=h​((v2,v3))−h​(())=1.2−0=1.2ℎconditionalsubscript𝑣2subscript𝑣3ℎsubscript𝑣2subscript𝑣3ℎ1.201.2h((v_{2},v_{3})|())=h((v_{2},v_{3}))-h(())=1.2-0=1.2 and h​((v2,v3)|(v1))=h​((v1,v2,v3))−h​((v1))=2.2−0.2=2ℎconditionalsubscript𝑣2subscript𝑣3subscript𝑣1ℎsubscript𝑣1subscript𝑣2subscript𝑣3ℎsubscript𝑣12.20.22h((v_{2},v_{3})|(v_{1}))=h((v_{1},v_{2},v_{3}))-h((v_{1}))=2.2-0.2=2.

In this section, we introduce some additional notations and present some important preliminary results that will be used in the proofs of Theorems 1, 2, and 3.

First, we restate the approximation performance of the SSG algorithm (presented in Algorithm 3) in [7]. Let sequence S𝑆S with |S|=k𝑆𝑘|S|=k be the one selected by the SSG algorithm, and let Sisuperscript𝑆𝑖S^{i} be the sequence consisting of the first i𝑖i elements of sequence S𝑆S with 1≤i≤k1𝑖𝑘1\leq i\leq k. Next, we state in Lemma 2 that the approximation performance of the SSG algorithm can be better characterized if the value of the selected sequence is concentrated in its first few elements. We present the proof of Lemma 2 in Appendix 8.4.

Suppose that function hℎh is forward-monotone, backward-monotone, and sequence-submodular (Assumption 1). In Case II-b: we have h​(S2)>h​(S2−{z})ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2𝑧h(S_{2})>h(S_{2}-\{z\}). Suppose k=2𝑘2k=2. Combining all the cases establishes an approximation ratio of Algorithm 1 and completes the proof. Specifically, combining the bounds in Eqs. (28), (29), and (31) (resp., (32)) yields the bound in Eq. (26a) (resp., (26b)).

We are now ready to prove Eqs. (30a)-(30d).

To prove Eq. (30a), we decompose it into two parts: (i) h​(S−{z})≥η⋅h​(S2)ℎ𝑆𝑧⋅𝜂ℎsubscript𝑆2h(S-\{z\})\geq\eta\cdot h(S_{2}) and (ii) h​(S−{z})≥(1−η)⋅h​(S2)ℎ𝑆𝑧⋅1𝜂ℎsubscript𝑆2h(S-\{z\})\geq(1-\eta)\cdot h(S_{2}).

Eq. (30b) holds trivially for any η∈(0,1]𝜂01\eta\in(0,1] by setting η𝜂\eta and 1−η1𝜂1-\eta to be equal and solving for η𝜂\eta.

Finally, we show that Eq. (30d) holds. We define two functions of η𝜂\eta:l1​(η)≜η⋅ek−2k−1−1ek−2k−1−η≜subscript𝑙1𝜂⋅𝜂superscript𝑒𝑘2𝑘11superscript𝑒𝑘2𝑘1𝜂l_{1}(\eta)\triangleq\eta\cdot\frac{e^{\frac{k-2}{k-1}}-1}{e^{\frac{k-2}{k-1}}-\eta} and l2​(η)≜(1−η)⋅ek−2k−1−1ek−2k−1−η≜subscript𝑙2𝜂⋅1𝜂superscript𝑒𝑘2𝑘11superscript𝑒𝑘2𝑘1𝜂l_{2}(\eta)\triangleq(1-\eta)\cdot\frac{e^{\frac{k-2}{k-1}}-1}{e^{\frac{k-2}{k-1}}-\eta}.It is easy to verify that for k>2𝑘2k>2 and η∈(0,1]𝜂01\eta\in(0,1], function l1​(η)subscript𝑙1𝜂l_{1}(\eta) is monotonically increasing and function l2​(η)subscript𝑙2𝜂l_{2}(\eta) is monotonically decreasing. Suppose that function hℎh is forward-monotone, backward-monotone, and sequence-submodular (Assumption 1). We use Lemmas 1, 2, and 3 presented in Appendix 8.3 to prove that Algorithm 1 achieves an approximation ratio of max⁡{(e−1)2e​(2​e−1),(e−1)​(ek−2​τk−τ−1)(2​e−1)​ek−2​τk−τ−(e−1)}superscript𝑒12𝑒2𝑒1𝑒1superscript𝑒𝑘2𝜏𝑘𝜏12𝑒1superscript𝑒𝑘2𝜏𝑘𝜏𝑒1\max\left\{\frac{(e-1)^{2}}{e(2e-1)},\frac{(e-1)(e^{\frac{k-2\tau}{k-\tau}}-1)}{(2e-1)e^{\frac{k-2\tau}{k-\tau}}-(e-1)}\right\} in the case of 1≤τ≤k1𝜏𝑘1\leq\tau\leq k, assuming the removal of τ𝜏\tau contiguous elements.

In Step 1 of Algorithm 1, it is equivalent that sequence S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} is selected by the SSG algorithm from set 𝒱𝒱\mathcal{V}, and we have |S1|=τsubscript𝑆1𝜏|S_{1}|=\tau. Similarly, in Step 2 of Algorithm 1, it is also equivalent that sequence S2subscript𝑆2S_{2} is selected by the SSG algorithm from set 𝒱∖𝒱​(S1)𝒱𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{V}\setminus\mathcal{V}(S_{1}), and we have |S2|=k−τsubscript𝑆2𝑘𝜏|S_{2}|=k-\tau. Hence, the sequence selected by Algorithm 1 can be written as S=S1⊕S2𝑆direct-sumsubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2S=S_{1}\oplus S_{2}. Recall that for any given sequence S𝑆S, set 𝒵τ​(S)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S) denotes the set of elements removed from sequence S𝑆S in the worst case (i.e., 𝒵τ​(S)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S) is an optimal solution to Problem (11)). We define 𝒵τ1​(S)≜𝒵τ​(S)∩𝒱​(S1)≜subscriptsuperscript𝒵1𝜏𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{Z}^{1}_{\tau}(S)\triangleq\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S)\cap\mathcal{V}(S_{1}) and 𝒵τ2​(S)≜𝒵τ​(S)∩𝒱​(S2)≜subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆𝒱subscript𝑆2\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\triangleq\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S)\cap\mathcal{V}(S_{2}) as the set of elements removed from subsequences S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} and S2subscript𝑆2S_{2}, respectively.

The proof of Theorem 2 follows a similar line of analysis as in the proof of Theorem 1 for the case of τ=1𝜏1\tau=1. Specifically, we will also consider three cases: (I) 𝒵τ2​(S)=∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)=\emptyset, (II-a) 𝒵τ2​(S)≠∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\neq\emptyset and h​(S2)≤h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆h(S_{2})\leq h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)), and (II-b) 𝒵τ2​(S)≠∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\neq\emptyset and h​(S2)>h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆h(S_{2})>h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)). The proofs of Case I and Case II-a are almost the same as those in Theorem 1, except for some minor technical differences. However, the proof of Case II-b is substantially different. The reason is the following. In the proof of Theorem 1, only one element can be removed from S𝑆S, and in Case II-b, this element has to be in 𝒱​(S2)𝒱subscript𝑆2\mathcal{V}(S_{2}), which makes it easier to characterize the impact of removing such an element. In the case of 1≤τ≤k1𝜏𝑘1\leq\tau\leq k, however, more than one element may be removed, which could be in either 𝒱​(S1)𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{V}(S_{1}) or 𝒱​(S2)𝒱subscript𝑆2\mathcal{V}(S_{2}) or both. Therefore, we present a different approach to address this new technical challenge.

We want to show the following two bounds that establish the approximation ratio of Algorithm 1:

h​(S−𝒵τ​(S))ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\displaystyle h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))≥(e−1)2e​(2​e−1)​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)),absentsuperscript𝑒12𝑒2𝑒1subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏\displaystyle\geq\frac{(e-1)^{2}}{e(2e-1)}g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)),(35a)h​(S−𝒵τ​(S))ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\displaystyle h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))≥(e−1)​(ek−2​τk−τ−1)(2​e−1)​ek−2​τk−τ−(e−1)​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)).absent𝑒1superscript𝑒𝑘2𝜏𝑘𝜏12𝑒1superscript𝑒𝑘2𝜏𝑘𝜏𝑒1subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏\displaystyle\geq\frac{(e-1)(e^{\frac{k-2\tau}{k-\tau}}-1)}{(2e-1)e^{\frac{k-2\tau}{k-\tau}}-(e-1)}g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)).(35b)

To begin with, we present a lower bound on h​(S2)ℎsubscript𝑆2h(S_{2}), which will be used throughout the proof:h​(S2)ℎsubscript𝑆2\displaystyle h(S_{2})≥(1−1/e)​h​(S∗​(𝒱∖𝒱​(S1),k−τ,0))absent11𝑒ℎsuperscript𝑆∗𝒱𝒱subscript𝑆1𝑘𝜏0\displaystyle\geq(1-1/e)h(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V}\setminus\mathcal{V}(S_{1}),k-\tau,0))(36)≥(1−1/e)​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)),absent11𝑒subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏\displaystyle\geq(1-1/e)g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)),where the first inequality is from Lemma 1 (where we replace 𝒱𝒱\mathcal{V}, k𝑘k, and i𝑖i with 𝒱∖𝒱​(S1)𝒱𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{V}\setminus\mathcal{V}(S_{1}), k−τ𝑘𝜏k-\tau, and k−τ𝑘𝜏k-\tau, respectively) and the second inequality is from Lemma 3 (where we replace 𝒱′superscript𝒱′\mathcal{V}^{\prime} with 𝒱​(S1)𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{V}(S_{1})).

The proof proceeds as follows. Elements in 𝒵τ​(S)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S) will be removed from sequence S𝑆S. These elements can be either fully or partially in 𝒱​(S1)𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{V}(S_{1}) (i.e., 𝒵τ2​(S)=∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)=\emptyset or 𝒵τ2​(S)≠∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\neq\emptyset). Therefore, we consider two cases: (I) 𝒵τ2​(S)=∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)=\emptyset and (II) 𝒵τ2​(S)≠∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\neq\emptyset.

In Case I, we have 𝒵τ2​(S)=∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)=\emptyset, i.e., 𝒵τ​(S)=𝒱​(S1)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S)=\mathcal{V}(S_{1}). Then, we have the following:h​(S−𝒵τ​(S))ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\displaystyle h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))=h​(S2)≥(1−1/e)​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)),absentℎsubscript𝑆211𝑒subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏\displaystyle=h(S_{2})\geq(1-1/e)g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)),(37)where the inequality follows from Eq. (36).

In Case II, we have 𝒵τ2​(S)≠∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\neq\emptyset.Depending on the impact of removing elements in 𝒵τ2​(S)subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S), we consider two subcases: (II-a) h​(S2)≤h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆h(S_{2})\leq h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)) and (II-b) h​(S2)>h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆h(S_{2})>h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)).

In Case II-a: we have h​(S2)≤h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆h(S_{2})\leq h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)). In this case, the removal of elements in 𝒵τ2​(S)subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S) does not reduce the overall value of the remaining sequence S2−𝒵τ2​(S)subscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S). Then, we haveh​(S−𝒵τ​(S))ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\displaystyle h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))=h​((S1−𝒵τ1​(S))⊕(S2−𝒵τ2​(S)))absentℎdirect-sumsubscript𝑆1subscriptsuperscript𝒵1𝜏𝑆subscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\displaystyle=h((S_{1}-\mathcal{Z}^{1}_{\tau}(S))\oplus(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)))(38)≥(a)h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))superscript(a)absentℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\displaystyle\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{(a)}}}{{\geq}}h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S))≥(b)h​(S2)superscript(b)absentℎsubscript𝑆2\displaystyle\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{(b)}}}{{\geq}}h(S_{2})≥(c)(1−1/e)​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)),superscript(c)absent11𝑒subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏\displaystyle\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{(c)}}}{{\geq}}(1-1/e)g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)),where (a) is due to the backward monotonicity of function hℎh, (b) holds from the condition of this subcase, and (c) follows from Eq. (36).

In Case II-b: we have h​(S2)>h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆h(S_{2})>h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)). Suppose k=τ+τ2𝑘𝜏subscript𝜏2k=\tau+\tau_{2}. Now, suppose k>τ+τ2𝑘𝜏subscript𝜏2k>\tau+\tau_{2}. Let η≜h​(S2)−h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))h​(S2)≜𝜂ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆ℎsubscript𝑆2\eta\triangleq\frac{h(S_{2})-h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S))}{h(S_{2})} denote the ratio of the loss of removing elements in 𝒵τ2​(S)subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S) from sequence S2subscript𝑆2S_{2} to the value of sequence S2subscript𝑆2S_{2}, and we have η∈(0,1]𝜂01\eta\in(0,1] due to h​(S2)>h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆h(S_{2})>h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)). We will prove Eqs. (40a)-(40d) later; for now, we assume that they all hold. Then, we can obtain the following bound:h​(S−𝒵τ​(S))ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\displaystyle h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))≥max⁡{η​(1−1/e)​h​(S2),(1−η)​h​(S2)}absent𝜂11𝑒ℎsubscript𝑆21𝜂ℎsubscript𝑆2\displaystyle\geq\max\{\eta(1-1/e)h(S_{2}),(1-\eta)h(S_{2})\}(41)≥max⁡{η​(1−1/e),(1−η)}⋅(1−1/e)​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ))absent⋅𝜂11𝑒1𝜂11𝑒subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏\displaystyle\geq\max\{\eta(1-1/e),(1-\eta)\}\cdot(1-1/e)g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau))≥(e−1)2e​(2​e−1)​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)),absentsuperscript𝑒12𝑒2𝑒1subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏\displaystyle\geq\frac{(e-1)^{2}}{e(2e-1)}g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)),where the three inequalities are from Eqs. (40a), (36), and (40b), respectively.

Similarly, we can also obtain the following bound:h​(S−𝒵τ​(S))ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\displaystyle h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))≥max⁡{η​(1−1/e)​h​(S2),(1−η)​h​(S2)}absent𝜂11𝑒ℎsubscript𝑆21𝜂ℎsubscript𝑆2\displaystyle\geq\max\{\eta(1-1/e)h(S_{2}),(1-\eta)h(S_{2})\}(42)≥max⁡{η​(e−1)​(ek−τ−τ2k−τ2−1)e​(ek−τ−τ2k−τ2−η​(1−1/e)),(1−η)​(ek−τ−τ2k−τ2−1)ek−τ−τ2k−τ2−η​(1−1/e)}​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ))absent𝜂𝑒1superscript𝑒𝑘𝜏subscript𝜏2𝑘subscript𝜏21𝑒superscript𝑒𝑘𝜏subscript𝜏2𝑘subscript𝜏2𝜂11𝑒1𝜂superscript𝑒𝑘𝜏subscript𝜏2𝑘subscript𝜏21superscript𝑒𝑘𝜏subscript𝜏2𝑘subscript𝜏2𝜂11𝑒subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏\displaystyle\geq\max\left\{\frac{\eta(e-1)(e^{\frac{k-\tau-\tau_{2}}{k-\tau_{2}}}-1)}{e(e^{\frac{k-\tau-\tau_{2}}{k-\tau_{2}}}-\eta(1-1/e))},\frac{(1-\eta)(e^{\frac{k-\tau-\tau_{2}}{k-\tau_{2}}}-1)}{e^{\frac{k-\tau-\tau_{2}}{k-\tau_{2}}}-\eta(1-1/e)}\right\}g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau))≥(e−1)​(ek−τ−τ2k−τ2−1)(2​e−1)​ek−τ−τ2k−τ2−(e−1)​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ))absent𝑒1superscript𝑒𝑘𝜏subscript𝜏2𝑘subscript𝜏212𝑒1superscript𝑒𝑘𝜏subscript𝜏2𝑘subscript𝜏2𝑒1subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏\displaystyle\geq\frac{(e-1)(e^{\frac{k-\tau-\tau_{2}}{k-\tau_{2}}}-1)}{(2e-1)e^{\frac{k-\tau-\tau_{2}}{k-\tau_{2}}}-(e-1)}g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau))≥(e−1)​(ek−2​τk−τ−1)(2​e−1)​ek−2​τk−τ−(e−1)​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)),absent𝑒1superscript𝑒𝑘2𝜏𝑘𝜏12𝑒1superscript𝑒𝑘2𝜏𝑘𝜏𝑒1subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏\displaystyle\geq\frac{(e-1)(e^{\frac{k-2\tau}{k-\tau}}-1)}{(2e-1)e^{\frac{k-2\tau}{k-\tau}}-(e-1)}g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)),where the first three inequalities are from Eqs. (40a), (40c), and (40d), respectively, and the last inequality is due to τ2≤τsubscript𝜏2𝜏\tau_{2}\leq\tau and that (e−1)​(ek−τ−τ2k−τ2−1)(2​e−1)​ek−τ−τ2k−τ2−(e−1)𝑒1superscript𝑒𝑘𝜏subscript𝜏2𝑘subscript𝜏212𝑒1superscript𝑒𝑘𝜏subscript𝜏2𝑘subscript𝜏2𝑒1\frac{(e-1)(e^{\frac{k-\tau-\tau_{2}}{k-\tau_{2}}}-1)}{(2e-1)e^{\frac{k-\tau-\tau_{2}}{k-\tau_{2}}}-(e-1)} is a decreasing function of τ2subscript𝜏2\tau_{2}.

Combining all the cases establishes the approximation ratios of Algorithm 1 and completes the proof. Specifically, combining the bounds in Eqs. (37), (38), (39), and (41) (resp., (42)) yields the bound in Eq. (35a) (resp., (35b)).

Now, it remains to show that Eqs. (40a)-(40d) hold in Case II-b, where we have 𝒵τ2​(S)≠∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\neq\emptyset and h​(S2)>h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆h(S_{2})>h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)). Recall that the τ𝜏\tau elements in 𝒵τ​(S)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S) form a contiguous subsequence of S𝑆S. Then, the elements in 𝒵τ1​(S)subscriptsuperscript𝒵1𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{1}_{\tau}(S) and 𝒵τ2​(S)subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S) also form a contiguous subsequence of S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} and S2subscript𝑆2S_{2}, respectively. We use Z1subscript𝑍1Z_{1} and Z2subscript𝑍2Z_{2} to denote the contiguous subsequence of elements in 𝒵τ1​(S)subscriptsuperscript𝒵1𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{1}_{\tau}(S) and 𝒵τ2​(S)subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S), respectively. We first rewrite S2subscript𝑆2S_{2} as S2=S21⊕Z2⊕S22subscript𝑆2direct-sumsuperscriptsubscript𝑆21subscript𝑍2superscriptsubscript𝑆22S_{2}=S_{2}^{1}\oplus Z_{2}\oplus S_{2}^{2}, where S21superscriptsubscript𝑆21S_{2}^{1} and S21superscriptsubscript𝑆21S_{2}^{1} denote the subsequences of S2subscript𝑆2S_{2} before and after subsequence Z2subscript𝑍2Z_{2}, respectively. Note that S21superscriptsubscript𝑆21S_{2}^{1} or S22superscriptsubscript𝑆22S_{2}^{2} could be an empty sequence, depending on the position of subsequence Z2subscript𝑍2Z_{2} in S2subscript𝑆2S_{2}. Suppose that function hℎh is forward-monotone, backward-monotone, and general-sequence-submodular (Assumption 2). We use Lemmas 1 and 3 presented in Appendix 8.3 to prove that Algorithm 2 achieves an approximation ratio of 1−1/e1+τ11𝑒1𝜏\frac{1-1/e}{1+\tau} in the case of 1≤τ≤k1𝜏𝑘1\leq\tau\leq k, assuming the removal of an arbitrary subset of τ𝜏\tau selected elements, which are not necessarily contiguous.

In Step 1 of Algorithm 2, sequence S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} is selected by choosing τ𝜏\tau elements with the highest individual values in a greedy manner, and we have |S1|=τsubscript𝑆1𝜏|S_{1}|=\tau. In Step 2 of Algorithm 1, it is equivalent that sequence S2subscript𝑆2S_{2} is selected by the SSG algorithm from set 𝒱∖𝒱​(S1)𝒱𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{V}\setminus\mathcal{V}(S_{1}), and we have |S2|=k−τsubscript𝑆2𝑘𝜏|S_{2}|=k-\tau. Hence, the sequence selected by Algorithm 1 can be written as S=S1⊕S2𝑆direct-sumsubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2S=S_{1}\oplus S_{2}. Recall that for any given sequence S𝑆S, set 𝒵τ​(S)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S) denotes the set of elements removed from sequence S𝑆S in the worst case (i.e., 𝒵τ​(S)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S) is an optimal solution to Problem (11)). We define 𝒵τ1​(S)≜𝒵τ​(S)∩𝒱​(S1)≜subscriptsuperscript𝒵1𝜏𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{Z}^{1}_{\tau}(S)\triangleq\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S)\cap\mathcal{V}(S_{1}) and 𝒵τ2​(S)≜𝒵τ​(S)∩𝒱​(S2)≜subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆𝒱subscript𝑆2\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\triangleq\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S)\cap\mathcal{V}(S_{2}) as the set of elements removed from subsequences S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} and S2subscript𝑆2S_{2}, respectively.

The proof of Theorem 3 follows a similar line of analysis as in the proof of Theorem 2. Specifically, we will also consider three cases: (I) 𝒵τ2​(S)=∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)=\emptyset, (II-a) 𝒵τ2​(S)≠∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\neq\emptyset and h​(S2)≤h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆h(S_{2})\leq h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)), and (II-b) 𝒵τ2​(S)≠∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\neq\emptyset and h​(S2)>h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆h(S_{2})>h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)). The proofs of Case I and Case II-a are identical to those in Theorem 2. Therefore, we focus on Case II-b, which requires a different proof strategy.

We want to show the following bound that establishes the approximation ratio of Algorithm 2:h​(S−𝒵τ​(S))≥1−1/e1+τ​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)).ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆11𝑒1𝜏subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))\geq\frac{1-1/e}{1+\tau}g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)).(48)

To begin with, we present a lower bound on h​(S2)ℎsubscript𝑆2h(S_{2}), which will be used throughout the proof:h​(S2)ℎsubscript𝑆2\displaystyle h(S_{2})≥(1−1/e)​h​(S∗​(𝒱∖𝒱​(S1),k−τ,0))absent11𝑒ℎsuperscript𝑆∗𝒱𝒱subscript𝑆1𝑘𝜏0\displaystyle\geq(1-1/e)h(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V}\setminus\mathcal{V}(S_{1}),k-\tau,0))(49)≥(1−1/e)​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)),absent11𝑒subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏\displaystyle\geq(1-1/e)g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)),where the first inequality is from Lemma 1 (where we replace 𝒱𝒱\mathcal{V}, k𝑘k, and i𝑖i with 𝒱∖𝒱​(S1)𝒱𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{V}\setminus\mathcal{V}(S_{1}), k−τ𝑘𝜏k-\tau, and k−τ𝑘𝜏k-\tau, respectively) and the second inequality is from Lemma 3 (where we replace 𝒱′superscript𝒱′\mathcal{V}^{\prime} with 𝒱​(S1)𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{V}(S_{1})).

We now focus on Case II-b, where we have 𝒵τ2​(S)≠∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\neq\emptyset and h​(S2)>h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆h(S_{2})>h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)). In this section, we generalize the results of Lemmas 1 and 2 under weaker assumptions based on approximate versions of sequence submodularity and backward monotonicity. Specifically, we introduce two weaker assumptions that will be used in this section.

We first generalize the approximation result of the SSG algorithm (Algorithm 3) under Assumption 6. Let sequence S𝑆S with |S|=k𝑆𝑘|S|=k be the one selected by the SSG algorithm, let Sisuperscript𝑆𝑖S^{i} be the sequence consisting of the first i𝑖i elements of sequence S𝑆S with 1≤i≤k1𝑖𝑘1\leq i\leq k. We borrow the result of [28] and restate a generalized version in Lemma 6. This is also a generalization of Lemma 1. While [28] assumes that function hℎh is forward-monotone, α𝛼\alpha-backward-monotone, and element-sequence-submodular and considers the case of i=k𝑖𝑘i=k only, the generalization to the result in Lemma 6 is fairly straightforward. Therefore, we refer the interested reader to [28] for the proof.

We also generalize the result of Lemma 2 and present the generalized result in Lemma 7. Note that the current proof techniques we use could only lead to the generalization under Assumption 7, which is slightly stronger than Assumption 6 (backward-monotone vs. α𝛼\alpha-backward-monotone).

Before we prove Lemma 7, we introduce two lemmas: Lemmas 8 and 9. Lemma 8 is a generalization of Lemma 4 and will be used in the proof of Lemma 9, which will be used in the proof of Lemma 7.

We use Lemma 3 presented in Appendix 8.3 and Lemmas 6 and 7 presented in Appendix 8.8 to prove the approximation ratios of Algorithm 1 in the case of τ=1𝜏1\tau=1. The proof follows a similar line of analysis as in the proof of Theorem 1.

Given τ=1𝜏1\tau=1, in Step 1 of Algorithm 1, the selected sequence S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} consists of one element only; this element is denoted by v1subscript𝑣1v_{1}, i.e., S1=(v1)subscript𝑆1subscript𝑣1S_{1}=(v_{1}). In Step 2 of Algorithm 1, it is equivalent that sequence S2subscript𝑆2S_{2} is selected by the SSG algorithm from set 𝒱∖{v1}𝒱subscript𝑣1\mathcal{V}\setminus\{v_{1}\}, and we have |S2|=k−τ=k−1subscript𝑆2𝑘𝜏𝑘1|S_{2}|=k-\tau=k-1. Hence, the sequence selected by Algorithm 1 can be written as S=S1⊕S2=(v1)⊕S2𝑆direct-sumsubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2direct-sumsubscript𝑣1subscript𝑆2S=S_{1}\oplus S_{2}=(v_{1})\oplus S_{2}. Recall that for any given sequence S𝑆S, set 𝒵τ​(S)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S) denotes the set of elements removed from sequence S𝑆S in the worst case (i.e., 𝒵τ​(S)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S) is an optimal solution to Problem (11)).Note that only one element will be removed from S𝑆S, i.e., |𝒵τ​(S)|=1subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆1|\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S)|=1. For ease of notation, we use z𝑧z to denote the only element in 𝒵τ​(S)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S), i.e., 𝒵τ​(S)={z}subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆𝑧\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S)=\{z\}.

We want to show the following two bounds, which establish the approximation ratios of Algorithm 1:1) Suppose that function hℎh is forward-monotone, backward-monotone, μ1subscript𝜇1\mu_{1}-element-sequence-submodular, and μ2subscript𝜇2\mu_{2}-sequence-submodular (Assumption 3), we haveh​(S−{z})≥a​(eb−1)eb−a​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)),where​a=μ1​μ2μ1+1​and​b=μ1​(k−2)k−1;formulae-sequenceℎ𝑆𝑧𝑎superscript𝑒𝑏1superscript𝑒𝑏𝑎subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏where𝑎subscript𝜇1subscript𝜇2subscript𝜇11and𝑏subscript𝜇1𝑘2𝑘1h(S-\{z\})\geq\frac{a(e^{b}-1)}{e^{b}-a}g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)),~{}\text{where}~{}a=\frac{\mu_{1}\mu_{2}}{\mu_{1}+1}~{}\text{and}~{}b=\frac{\mu_{1}(k-2)}{k-1};\\(63)2) Suppose that function hℎh is forward-monotone, α𝛼\alpha-backward-monotone, μ1subscript𝜇1\mu_{1}-element-sequence-submodular, and μ2subscript𝜇2\mu_{2}-sequence-submodular (Assumption 4), we haveh​(S−{z})≥α2​μ1​μ2​(eμ1−1)(μ1+α)​eμ1​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)).ℎ𝑆𝑧superscript𝛼2subscript𝜇1subscript𝜇2superscript𝑒subscript𝜇11subscript𝜇1𝛼superscript𝑒subscript𝜇1subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏h(S-\{z\})\geq\frac{\alpha^{2}\mu_{1}\mu_{2}(e^{\mu_{1}}-1)}{(\mu_{1}+\alpha)e^{\mu_{1}}}g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)).(64)

The proof proceeds as follows. Element z𝑧z is an element that will be removed, which can be either v1subscript𝑣1v_{1} or an element in S2subscript𝑆2S_{2}. Therefore, we consider two cases: (I) z=v1𝑧subscript𝑣1z=v_{1} and (II) z≠v1𝑧subscript𝑣1z\neq v_{1}.

Next, we prove Eq. (68a). Eq. (68b) holds trivially for any η∈(0,1]𝜂01\eta\in(0,1] by setting μ1​(η+μ2−1)subscript𝜇1𝜂subscript𝜇21\mu_{1}(\eta+\mu_{2}-1) and α​(1−η)𝛼1𝜂\alpha(1-\eta) to be equal, solving for η𝜂\eta, and plugging it back. This completes the proof of the bound in Eq. (64).

Next, we prove the bound in Eq. (63). Note that the analysis so far applies to function hℎh that is forward-monotone, α𝛼\alpha-backward-monotone, μ1subscript𝜇1\mu_{1}-element-sequence-submodular, and μ2subscript𝜇2\mu_{2}-sequence-submodular (Assumption 4), and hence, it also applies to Assumption 3, which is a special case of Assumption 4 with α=1𝛼1\alpha=1. The bound in Eq. (63) requires backward monotonicity (i.e., α=1𝛼1\alpha=1), but it becomes better than the bound in Eq. (64) when k𝑘k is large. In the following analysis, we assume that function hℎh is forward-monotone, backward-monotone, μ1subscript𝜇1\mu_{1}-element-sequence-submodular, and μ2subscript𝜇2\mu_{2}-sequence-submodular (Assumption 3).

We use Lemma 3 presented in Appendix 8.3 and Lemmas 6 and 7 presented in Appendix 8.8 to prove the approximation ratios of Algorithm 1 in the case of 1≤τ≤k1𝜏𝑘1\leq\tau\leq k, assuming the removal of τ𝜏\tau contiguous elements. The proof follows a similar line of analysis as in the proof of Theorem 2.

In Step 1 of Algorithm 1, it is equivalent that sequence S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} is selected by the SSG algorithm from set 𝒱𝒱\mathcal{V}, and we have |S1|=τsubscript𝑆1𝜏|S_{1}|=\tau. Similarly, in Step 2 of Algorithm 1, it is also equivalent that sequence S2subscript𝑆2S_{2} is selected by the SSG algorithm from set 𝒱∖𝒱​(S1)𝒱𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{V}\setminus\mathcal{V}(S_{1}), and we have |S2|=k−τsubscript𝑆2𝑘𝜏|S_{2}|=k-\tau. Hence, the sequence selected by Algorithm 1 can be written as S=S1⊕S2𝑆direct-sumsubscript𝑆1subscript𝑆2S=S_{1}\oplus S_{2}. Recall that for any given sequence S𝑆S, set 𝒵τ​(S)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S) denotes the set of elements removed from sequence S𝑆S in the worst case (i.e., 𝒵τ​(S)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S) is an optimal solution to Problem (11)). We define 𝒵τ1​(S)≜𝒵τ​(S)∩𝒱​(S1)≜subscriptsuperscript𝒵1𝜏𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{Z}^{1}_{\tau}(S)\triangleq\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S)\cap\mathcal{V}(S_{1}) and 𝒵τ2​(S)≜𝒵τ​(S)∩𝒱​(S2)≜subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆𝒱subscript𝑆2\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\triangleq\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S)\cap\mathcal{V}(S_{2}) as the set of elements removed from subsequences S1subscript𝑆1S_{1} and S2subscript𝑆2S_{2}, respectively.

We want to show the following two bounds, which establish the approximation ratios of Algorithm 1:1) Suppose that function hℎh is forward-monotone, backward-monotone, μ1subscript𝜇1\mu_{1}-element-sequence-submodular, and μ2subscript𝜇2\mu_{2}-sequence-submodular (Assumption 3), we haveh​(S−𝒵τ​(S))≥a​μ2​(eb−1)(a+1)​eb−a​μ2​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)),ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆𝑎subscript𝜇2superscript𝑒𝑏1𝑎1superscript𝑒𝑏𝑎subscript𝜇2subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))\geq\frac{a\mu_{2}(e^{b}-1)}{(a+1)e^{b}-a\mu_{2}}g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)),(75)where a=μ1⋅(1−1/eμ1)𝑎⋅subscript𝜇111superscript𝑒subscript𝜇1a=\mu_{1}\cdot(1-1/e^{\mu_{1}}) and b=μ1⋅k−2​τk−τ𝑏⋅subscript𝜇1𝑘2𝜏𝑘𝜏b=\mu_{1}\cdot\frac{k-2\tau}{k-\tau};2) Suppose that function hℎh is forward-monotone, α𝛼\alpha-backward-monotone, μ1subscript𝜇1\mu_{1}-element-sequence-submodular, and μ2subscript𝜇2\mu_{2}-sequence-submodular (Assumption 4), we haveh​(S−𝒵τ​(S))≥α2​μ1​μ2​(eμ1−1)2μ1​eμ1​(eμ1−1)+e2​μ1​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)).ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆superscript𝛼2subscript𝜇1subscript𝜇2superscriptsuperscript𝑒subscript𝜇112subscript𝜇1superscript𝑒subscript𝜇1superscript𝑒subscript𝜇11superscript𝑒2subscript𝜇1subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))\geq\frac{\alpha^{2}\mu_{1}\mu_{2}(e^{\mu_{1}}-1)^{2}}{\mu_{1}e^{\mu_{1}}(e^{\mu_{1}}-1)+e^{2\mu_{1}}}g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)).(76)

We begin with the proof of Eq. (76) as some of the intermediate results will be used in the proof of Eq. (75).We first present a lower bound on h​(S2)ℎsubscript𝑆2h(S_{2}), which will be used throughout the proof:h​(S2)ℎsubscript𝑆2\displaystyle h(S_{2})≥α​(1−1/eμ1)​h​(S∗​(𝒱∖𝒱​(S1),k−τ,0))absent𝛼11superscript𝑒subscript𝜇1ℎsuperscript𝑆∗𝒱𝒱subscript𝑆1𝑘𝜏0\displaystyle\geq\alpha(1-1/e^{\mu_{1}})h(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V}\setminus\mathcal{V}(S_{1}),k-\tau,0))(77)≥α​(1−1/eμ1)​gτ​(S∗​(𝒱,k,τ)),absent𝛼11superscript𝑒subscript𝜇1subscript𝑔𝜏superscript𝑆∗𝒱𝑘𝜏\displaystyle\geq\alpha(1-1/e^{\mu_{1}})g_{\tau}(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},k,\tau)),where the first inequality is from Lemma 6 (where we replace 𝒱𝒱\mathcal{V}, k𝑘k, and i𝑖i with 𝒱∖𝒱​(S1)𝒱𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{V}\setminus\mathcal{V}(S_{1}), k−τ𝑘𝜏k-\tau, and k−τ𝑘𝜏k-\tau, respectively) and the second inequality is from Lemma 3 (where we replace 𝒱′superscript𝒱′\mathcal{V}^{\prime} with 𝒱​(S1)𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{V}(S_{1})).

The proof proceeds as follows. Elements in 𝒵τ​(S)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S) will be removed from sequence S𝑆S. These elements can be either fully or partially in 𝒱​(S1)𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{V}(S_{1}) (i.e., 𝒵τ2​(S)=∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)=\emptyset or 𝒵τ2​(S)≠∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\neq\emptyset). Therefore, we consider two cases: (I) 𝒵τ2​(S)=∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)=\emptyset and (II) 𝒵τ2​(S)≠∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\neq\emptyset.

In Case I, we have 𝒵τ2​(S)=∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)=\emptyset, i.e., 𝒵τ​(S)=𝒱​(S1)subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆𝒱subscript𝑆1\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S)=\mathcal{V}(S_{1}). In Case II, we have 𝒵τ2​(S)≠∅subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)\neq\emptyset.Depending on the impact of removing elements in 𝒵τ2​(S)subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S), we consider two subcases: (II-a) h​(S2)≤h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆h(S_{2})\leq h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)) and (II-b) h​(S2)>h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))ℎsubscript𝑆2ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆h(S_{2})>h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)).

To prove Eq. (80a), we decompose it into two parts: (i) h​(S−𝒵τ​(S))≥μ1​α​(η+μ2−1)​(1−1/eμ1)⋅h​(S2)ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆⋅subscript𝜇1𝛼𝜂subscript𝜇2111superscript𝑒subscript𝜇1ℎsubscript𝑆2h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))\geq\mu_{1}\alpha(\eta+\mu_{2}-1)(1-1/e^{\mu_{1}})\cdot h(S_{2}) and (ii) h​(S−𝒵τ​(S))≥α​(1−η)⋅h​(S2)ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆⋅𝛼1𝜂ℎsubscript𝑆2h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))\geq\alpha(1-\eta)\cdot h(S_{2}).

Let τ2≜|𝒵τ2​(S)|≜subscript𝜏2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\tau_{2}\triangleq|\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)|. Then, Part (i) can be shown through the following:h​(S−𝒵τ​(S))ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆\displaystyle h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))=h​((S1−𝒵τ1​(S))⊕(S2−𝒵τ2​(S)))absentℎdirect-sumsubscript𝑆1subscriptsuperscript𝒵1𝜏𝑆subscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆\displaystyle=h((S_{1}-\mathcal{Z}^{1}_{\tau}(S))\oplus(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)))(84)≥(a)h​(S1−𝒵τ1​(S))superscript(a)absentℎsubscript𝑆1subscriptsuperscript𝒵1𝜏𝑆\displaystyle\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{(a)}}}{{\geq}}h(S_{1}-\mathcal{Z}^{1}_{\tau}(S))=h​(S1τ2)absentℎsuperscriptsubscript𝑆1subscript𝜏2\displaystyle=h(S_{1}^{\tau_{2}})≥(b)α​(1−1/eμ1)⋅h​(S∗​(𝒱,τ2,0))superscript(b)absent⋅𝛼11superscript𝑒subscript𝜇1ℎsuperscript𝑆∗𝒱subscript𝜏20\displaystyle\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{(b)}}}{{\geq}}\alpha(1-1/e^{\mu_{1}})\cdot h(S^{\ast}(\mathcal{V},\tau_{2},0))≥(c)α​(1−1/eμ1)⋅h​(Z2)superscript(c)absent⋅𝛼11superscript𝑒subscript𝜇1ℎsubscript𝑍2\displaystyle\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{(c)}}}{{\geq}}\alpha(1-1/e^{\mu_{1}})\cdot h(Z_{2})≥(d)μ1​α​(η+μ2−1)​(1−1/eμ1)⋅h​(S2),superscript(d)absent⋅subscript𝜇1𝛼𝜂subscript𝜇2111superscript𝑒subscript𝜇1ℎsubscript𝑆2\displaystyle\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{(d)}}}{{\geq}}\mu_{1}\alpha(\eta+\mu_{2}-1)(1-1/e^{\mu_{1}})\cdot h(S_{2}),where (a) is due to the forward monotonicity of function hℎh, (b) is due to the greedy manner of selecting subsequence S1τ2superscriptsubscript𝑆1subscript𝜏2S_{1}^{\tau_{2}} from set 𝒱𝒱\mathcal{V} and Lemma 6 (where we replace both k𝑘k and i𝑖i with τ2subscript𝜏2\tau_{2}), (c) holds because sequence Z2subscript𝑍2Z_{2} is a feasible solution to Problem (P𝑃P) for selecting a sequence of τ2subscript𝜏2\tau_{2} elements from 𝒱𝒱\mathcal{V}, and (d) is from Eq. (83).

Eq. (80b) holds trivially for any η∈(0,1]𝜂01\eta\in(0,1] by setting μ1​α​(η+μ2−1)​(1−1/eμ1)subscript𝜇1𝛼𝜂subscript𝜇2111superscript𝑒subscript𝜇1\mu_{1}\alpha(\eta+\mu_{2}-1)(1-1/e^{\mu_{1}}) and α​(1−η)𝛼1𝜂\alpha(1-\eta) to be equal, solving for η𝜂\eta, and plugging it back. Now, we prove the bound in Eq. (75). Note that the analysis so far applies to any function hℎh that is forward-monotone, α𝛼\alpha-backward-monotone, μ1subscript𝜇1\mu_{1}-element-sequence-submodular, and μ2subscript𝜇2\mu_{2}-sequence-submodular (Assumption 4), and hence, it also applies to Assumption 3, which is a special case of Assumption 4 with α=1𝛼1\alpha=1. The bound in Eq. (75) requires backward monotonicity (i.e., α=1𝛼1\alpha=1), but it becomes better than the bound in Eq. (76) when k𝑘k is large.In the following analysis, we assume that function hℎh is forward-monotone, backward-monotone, μ1subscript𝜇1\mu_{1}-element-sequence-submodular, and μ2subscript𝜇2\mu_{2}-sequence-submodular (Assumption 3).

The proof proceeds as follows. We borrow the analysis of Case-I, Case II-a, and Eq. (80a) from the previous analysis by setting α=1𝛼1\alpha=1. Then, for Case-II-b, we provide a different analysis.

Suppose that function hℎh is forward-monotone, α𝛼\alpha-backward-monotone, μ1subscript𝜇1\mu_{1}-element-sequence-submodular, and μ3subscript𝜇3\mu_{3}-general-sequence-submodular (Assumption 5). The proof of Theorem 6 follows a similar line of analysis as in the proof of Theorem 5. To prove Eq. (93a), we decompose it into two parts: (i) h​(S−𝒵τ​(S))≥μ1​(η+μ3−1)τ⋅h​(S2)ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆⋅subscript𝜇1𝜂subscript𝜇31𝜏ℎsubscript𝑆2h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))\geq\frac{\mu_{1}(\eta+\mu_{3}-1)}{\tau}\cdot h(S_{2}) and (ii) h​(S−𝒵τ​(S))≥α​(1−η)⋅h​(S2)ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆⋅𝛼1𝜂ℎsubscript𝑆2h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))\geq\alpha(1-\eta)\cdot h(S_{2}).

Part (ii) can be shown through the following:h​(S−𝒵τ​(S))=h​((S1−𝒵τ1​(S))⊕(S2−𝒵τ2​(S)))≥(a)α⋅h​(S2−𝒵τ2​(S))=(b)α​(1−η)⋅h​(S2),ℎ𝑆subscript𝒵𝜏𝑆ℎdirect-sumsubscript𝑆1subscriptsuperscript𝒵1𝜏𝑆subscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆superscript(a)⋅𝛼ℎsubscript𝑆2subscriptsuperscript𝒵2𝜏𝑆superscript(b)⋅𝛼1𝜂ℎsubscript𝑆2\displaystyle h(S-\mathcal{Z}_{\tau}(S))=h((S_{1}-\mathcal{Z}^{1}_{\tau}(S))\oplus(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S)))\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{(a)}}}{{\geq}}\alpha\cdot h(S_{2}-\mathcal{Z}^{2}_{\tau}(S))\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{(b)}}}{{=}}\alpha(1-\eta)\cdot h(S_{2}),where (a) is from the α𝛼\alpha-backward monotonicity of function hℎh and (b) is from the definition of η𝜂\eta.

Eq. (93b) holds trivially for any η∈(0,1]𝜂01\eta\in(0,1] by setting μ1​(η+μ3−1)τsubscript𝜇1𝜂subscript𝜇31𝜏\frac{\mu_{1}(\eta+\mu_{3}-1)}{\tau} and α​(1−η)𝛼1𝜂\alpha(1-\eta) to be equal, solving for η𝜂\eta, and plugging it back. This completes the proof.∎
